# Chapter 23: What the Key Changed

The rain came back on the fifth day.

Not dramatically. It arrived during the hour when the market was full and the women had to choose between shelter and the last of the good fish, which meant the town remembered it not as a storm but as an inconvenience. The protagonist heard it first on the roof, a sound so ordinary that he almost did not recognise it as the thing they had been waiting for.

He was at the table. The rent book was closed. The red thread was still there, beside it, exactly where Obianuju had left it four days ago. She had not moved it. She had not put it away. She had left it on the table as though the table were the only place where the loosening could be watched without being touched.

The rain increased.

He stood and walked to the back of the house. The sound of water on the lean-to roof was different from the sound of water on the main roof. He needed to know which sound the house was making.

The lean-to door was closed.

The chalk line was still there.

He had checked it every morning for four days. Each time, the line was unbroken. Each time, he had felt relief, then shame at the relief, because the line's survival was not his doing. He had not drawn it. He had not braced the wood. He had only held a key for one morning and returned it with the thread loosened.

Now the rain was testing what he had not done.

He stood in the side passage with the wet hitting the ground near his feet and the sound of water filling the narrow space between the walls. The chalk line was visible from where he stood. It was still intact. But the wood around it had darkened, and the line was harder to see against the wet, and he understood that visibility was a kind of protection that rain could take away.

He went inside.

Obianuju was in the kitchen. She had not asked where he had been. She did not ask now. She was shelling beans into a bowl, her fingers moving with the same economy they had always had, and the bowl was the same bowl, and the beans were the same beans, and the ordinary rhythm of her hands was the only thing in the house that had not changed.

"The rain is here," he said.

"I know."

"The chalk line — "

"I know."

She did not look up. Her fingers continued their work. A bean shell cracked, split, fell. The pale insides dropped into the bowl with a small dry sound that the rain could not cover.

He sat down across from her. He picked up a bean from the pile. He had never shelled beans with her before — not once in two years. He had watched her do it a hundred times, had sat across from her while her fingers moved, but he had never joined her. He had thought the watching was enough.

He cracked the shell. It broke badly, fragments scattering, the inside splitting where it should have stayed whole.

She looked at his hands.

"Like this," she said.

She took a bean and showed him — the seam, the pressure, the place where the thumb finds the natural break. She did not make it a lesson. She simply did it, and he watched, and then he tried again.

The second shell cracked cleanly.

She nodded. Once. That was all.

He placed the pale insides in the bowl beside hers. The rain filled the silence between them, and the beans accumulated, and for a few minutes the house was only a house and the cold was only a word he had used too often.

---

The red thread was still on the table when they finished.

She picked it up. She held it between her fingers, the same way she had held the key four days ago, and she looked at it the way she had looked at the chalk line when it was first drawn — not with fear, not with anger, but with the attention of a woman who had learned that small things could carry more weight than large ones.

"The thread loosened," she said.

"I know."

"Not because you touched it. Because the key was used in a lock it did not belong to. The key knew. The thread knew. The house knew."

He said nothing.

"I have been thinking about what that means," she said. "For four days. While you checked the chalk line every morning, I have been sitting with the thread."

She held it out to him.

He did not take it.

"Take it," she said.

He took it.

The thread was dry. It was lighter than he remembered, and the red had faded in the places where the knots had been. He could see the shape of the old knots in the fibre, the slight crimp where the thread had been tight and was now loose.

"What does it mean?" he asked.

"It means the house accepted the wrong key."

The sentence found the place in him that had no answer and stayed there.

"The house accepted it," he said.

"Yes."

"But the key did not open the room."

"No."

"Then what did it open?"

She looked at him. Her face was not unkind. Her shoulders had settled into the shape they took when she had been holding something for a long time and had decided to put it down.

"It opened a path," she said. "Not a door. A path. The wrong key in the wrong lock does not open the door. But it shows the house that a key exists. It shows the house that someone is trying. And the house, after enough trying, begins to prepare for the possibility of being opened."

He stood with the thread in his hand. The rain was steady now, a grey curtain between the house and the town, and the light in the front room had gone the colour of old paper.

*You can be welcomed and still be late.*

The sentence came back to him without ceremony. Not as comfort. Not as wisdom. As accusation. He had been late to the house, late to the door, late to the cost, and now even his waiting had begun to arrive late. The house had accepted the wrong key while he was still learning how to hold the right one.

"What do we do?" he asked.

"We wait."

"For what?"

"For the next rain."

---

The next rain came sooner than expected.

Not the next day. The day after that. A second storm, smaller than the first, arriving in the late afternoon when the light was already failing. The protagonist heard it coming before it arrived — the change in the air, the way the market sounds shifted as people began to cover their goods and call their children. He was at the gate when the first drops fell, and he saw Mr Stephen before Mr Stephen saw him.

He was standing at the end of the lane, under the awning of the tailor's shop, holding an umbrella that was not yet open. He was not walking toward the house. He was not walking away from it. He was standing where he could see the gate without appearing to watch it, and the umbrella in his hand was the kind of detail that could be explained if anyone asked.

The protagonist stepped back into the yard.

He did not call out. He did not signal. He went inside and stood in the front room with his back to the window, and he waited for the feeling to pass — the feeling that the house was no longer private, that the lane was no longer ordinary, that a man with an umbrella and a notebook and a reasonable manner had begun to treat the weather as an appointment.

Obianuju came in from the kitchen.

"Mr Stephen is at the end of the lane," he said.

She did not ask how he knew. She walked to the window and looked out, standing far enough back that her face would not be visible from the lane. She watched for a long moment. Then she turned.

"He is not coming today."

"How do you know?"

"Because he is waiting for us to see him and react. If we react, he knows the chalk line matters. If we do not react, he has to find another way to measure what he gained."

She returned to the kitchen.

The protagonist stayed at the window.

He watched Mr Stephen stand under the awning for another ten minutes. Then the rain eased, and Mr Stephen closed his umbrella and walked away without once looking at the house.

That was when the protagonist did what he had not planned to do.

He went to the lean-to. He took the chalk from his pocket — he had picked it up from the table where Brother Paul had left it, without thinking, or thinking too much — and he drew a second line beside the first. Not parallel. Not straight. A line that looked like an accident, like someone had brushed against the wet wood and left a mark.

He did not know why he did it.

He knew immediately that it was wrong.

The second line would be visible to Mr Stephen. It would say: someone has been here since Brother Paul left. Someone is watching the line the way Mr Stephen is watching the house. It would say: the chalk line matters.

He stared at what he had done.

The rain fell on the new line. The chalk began to blur.

He could not undo it.

---

Brother Paul came on the seventh day.

Not because they had sent for him. Because the rain had stopped and the sun had returned, and he was a man who finished what he started when the weather allowed it. He arrived at the gate with the same canvas roll of tools, the same practical expression, and a new piece of chalk in his pocket.

"The brace held," he said.

"Yes."

"Good. I will check the line and reinforce if needed."

He walked around the side of the house. The protagonist followed. Brother Paul crouched by the lean-to frame and stopped.

"There are two lines," he said.

"I know."

Brother Paul looked at him. "Did you draw this?"

"Yes."

Brother Paul waited for an explanation. The protagonist did not give one, because he did not have one that would make sense to a repairman.

"Don't draw on the wood," Brother Paul said. "It confuses the measurement."

He rubbed out the second line with his thumb. The chalk smeared and disappeared, and the first line was left alone — curved, intact, the only one that meant anything.

The protagonist watched the second line vanish. The chalk dust settled into the wet grain of the wood. In a few minutes, the rain would wash it away entirely. His mark had lasted less than a day. He had acted, and the acting had been wiped away by a repairman with his thumb, and the only thing left was the shame of having marked something he did not understand.

"Don't draw on the wood," Brother Paul said. "It confuses the measurement."

The protagonist nodded. He did not trust himself to speak. He had wanted to leave a mark. He had left one. It had lasted less than a day. That was his first unguided action in this house: a mark that a repairman erased with his thumb.

Brother Paul redrew the first line in a fresh straight stroke. "There. Now we know."

He did not ask again. He was a practical man, and practical men do not chase what they cannot measure.

---

Kene arrived in the late afternoon, after Brother Paul had left, after the chalk line had been redrawn. He came with fish wrapped in newspaper and a question he did not ask until he had eaten half of it.

"The chalk line moved," Kene said.

"Brother Paul said the wood moved around the brace."

"Brother Paul is a repairman. He sees wood. What do you see?"

The protagonist looked at his hands.

"I see a line that was straight and is now curved. I see a lock that has been oiled. I see a man with an umbrella standing at the end of a lane, waiting to see whether we notice him."

Kene tore a piece of fish and chewed it slowly.

"That is a good answer. It is also a terrible answer, because it means you are seeing the right things and cannot do anything about them."

"I did something."

Kene looked at him.

"I drew a second chalk line. Brother Paul erased it."

Kene closed his eyes. "What did you think it would do?"

"I do not know."

"That is the problem with people who are not used to acting. When they finally act, they do it without knowing what the action means."

The protagonist said nothing.

"Did Obianuju see it?"

"No."

"Good. Do not tell her."

"Why?"

"Because she has enough to carry without adding your experiments."

---

The woman who had sold Mr Stephen the house lived three lanes over, in a house with a blue door that had been painted recently. The protagonist found her sitting on her front step, shelling groundnuts into a tin bowl.

"You are the man from the cold house," she said.

"Yes."

"Mr Stephen sent you?"

"No."

She considered this. She shelled three groundnuts while she considered it.

"Then you came on your own."

"Yes."

"That is better. Not much better. But better."

She told him, then, why she had sold the house. Not the full history — she gave him the shape of it, the way a person gives a thing she has carried so long that the edges have worn smooth. She said the cold had been there before her, that she had not known until it was too late, that she had sold to Mr Stephen because he was the only person who did not ask why she was leaving.

"He asked about the roof," she said. "He asked about the back door. He did not ask about the cold. I thought that meant he did not know. Now I think it meant he knew enough to wait."

"Does he know what it is?"

"He knows something. He does not know what it is. But he knows it is there, and he is trying to find out how much."

"How much is it worth?"

She shelled another groundnut. The shell cracked and fell into the bowl at her feet.

"That depends on whether you are buying or selling."

---

He walked home with the sentence in him. The rain had stopped. The evening light was the particular gold it went only in the half-hour before the calls, and the town was doing its ordinary evening — women calling children, radios playing music, the smell of plantain frying and diesel and salt.

He reached the house and stood at the gate.

Obianuju was in the front room, sitting in the chair by the window. She was looking at the red thread, which she had picked up from the table and was holding between her fingers.

"I went to see the woman who sold Mr Stephen the house," he said.

"I know."

"She told me that Mr Stephen knows something is in the house. He does not know what it is. But he knows it is there, and he is trying to find out how much it is worth."

Obianuju was quiet for a long moment. Then she said, "That is what I was afraid of."

"That he would find out?"

"That he would find out before I understood what I was protecting."

She stood up. She walked to the no-colour door. She put her hand on the handle, and he saw that her hand was not shaking. That was worse than if it had been.

"Mr Stephen is not going to open this door. He does not have the key. He does not know what is on the other side. But he knows there is something, and he is willing to wait for it. And waiting, for a man like him, is not passivity. It is preparation."

"What do we do?"

She looked at him. Her jaw had closed the way it closed before she made a decision. She held the red thread as though it was something she had agreed to carry.

"We make the door less worth opening."

"How?"

She held up the red thread.

"By giving him something else to find."

"What?"

She did not answer at once. She walked to the shelf where the old cloth pouch hung — the one that had held the key before the red thread was tied to it. She took it down. She opened it. She removed a small object he had never seen before.

It was a photograph. Folded once, the crease yellowed, the image faded. She did not show it to him. She held it in her hand the way she had held the thread — not with fear, not with anger, but with the attention of a woman who had learned that small things could carry more weight than large ones.

"This is what I will give him," she said.

"What is it?"

"Something that belongs to the house. Not the room. The house. Something he can find without breaking anything. Something that will make him think he has found what he was looking for."

"Will it work?"

She looked at the photograph. Her face was unreadable.

"It will work long enough."

She placed the photograph on the table, beside the red thread. She did not put it away. She left it there, where it could be seen, where it could be found, where it could be given.

The protagonist looked at the photograph. He did not touch it. He did not ask to see it. He understood that she was choosing among losses, and that this was the smallest loss she could afford.

---

That night, after Obianuju had gone to bed, he sat in the front room with the lamp burning low. The photograph was on the table. The red thread was beside it. The cold was at the edges of the room, waiting, patient, unhurried.

He thought of the second chalk line he had drawn, the one Brother Paul had erased. He thought of the woman with the blue door, who had left something in the house and was not sure whether it had stayed or followed her. He thought of Mr Stephen, who had the weather on his side and did not need to hurry.

He thought of Obianuju, who had placed a photograph on the table — a piece of her history, surrendered to be found.

He did not know what the photograph showed. He did not ask to see it. He understood that asking would make her explain, and explaining would cost her more than the giving already had. It was a picture of someone, or somewhere, or something that belonged to the house before he arrived, before she arrived, before the cold had a name. It was a piece of the past that she had kept hidden, and she was now willing to let Mr Stephen find it so that he would stop looking for the present.

He thought of what it meant to give away a piece of your history to protect what you still had. He thought of the woman with the blue door, who had given away the whole house to protect herself. He thought of Obianuju, who was giving away less but also more — a memory, a secret, a piece of herself that she had kept folded and hidden for years.

He did not know whether it would work. He did not know whether Mr Stephen would be satisfied with a photograph when what he really wanted was the room. But he knew that Obianuju had chosen this, and that the choosing had cost her something real, and that he would help her give it because the giving was the only way to keep the door closed.

He stood up.

He walked to the no-colour door and put his hand on the handle. The handle was cold. Not the cold of metal left in a draught. The cold of something that had been touched by a room it did not belong to.

He did not open the door.

He stood there with his hand on the cold handle. The door was not the door he needed to open. The one he needed to open was the one that would let him stop waiting and start helping her give what she had chosen to give.

He took his hand off the handle.

He went back to the front room.

He sat down.

The photograph was on the table.

The red thread was beside it.

The cold was at the edges.

And in the morning, Mr Stephen would receive something he had not asked for — a piece of the house, surrendered, not because it would satisfy him, but because it would slow him down long enough for the house to decide what to do next.
MOVEMENT II DRAFT. Ch.24: The Photograph. Function: the photograph alters the endgame. Obianuju controls the terms of exposure. Mr Stephen receives more than he asked for. The protagonist carries without owning. The room remains closed but danger moves onto the table. No new Trance. No new Voice line. Motifs: photograph/glass/table/hands/frame/threshold. Target 3,500-4,500.
-->

# Chapter 24: The Photograph

The photograph was still on the table when he woke.

He had not expected it to be. He had expected her to put it away before dawn, to reconsider, to choose a different loss. But the photograph was there, in the same place she had left it, face-down, the folded back showing the yellowed crease and a faint ghost of whatever image the paper had held against itself for years.

He did not touch it.

He stood in the doorway of the front room and looked at it. He needed to see whether the danger had a shape he could recognise.

It did not.

It was only paper. Folded once. The size of a hand. The edges softened by years of being held and put away, held and put away, held and finally hidden.

Obianuju came in from the kitchen. She did not look at the photograph. She looked at him standing in the doorway, and she read his hesitation the way she read everything — without appearing to read, without missing anything.

"It is still there," she said.

"Yes."

"It will stay there until he comes."

"And after?"

She did not answer. She walked to the table and stood over the photograph. She did not pick it up. She looked down at it the way a woman looks at a thing she has chosen to give away — not with regret, not with resolve, but with the attention of someone who has already done the mourning and is now only waiting for the moment of release.

"After, it will be his."

The sentence was plain. It was not dramatic. It was the plainest thing she had said all morning, and it hurt more than anything else she could have said.

He wanted to ask her again what the photograph showed. He wanted to ask who the woman was, and why her face was worth keeping hidden for years, and what it cost to place it on a table where a man like Mr Stephen could pick it up. But he did not ask, because he understood that asking would make her explain, and explaining would cost her more than the giving already had.

Instead, he said, "What do you need me to do?"

She looked at him. Her face did not soften, but something in it shifted — not trust, not gratitude, but a kind of acknowledgment that he had asked the right question instead of the wrong one.

"When he comes, you will stand at the door. You will not leave the front room. You will not speak unless I ask you to. You will not look at the photograph while he is looking at it."

"Not look at it?"

"If you look at it while he is holding it, he will see that you have not seen it before. He will know that I have kept it from you. And he will understand that the photograph matters more than I am letting him see."

He understood. He did not like it, but he understood. She was asking him to perform ignorance — to stand in the room while a stranger held a piece of her history, and to act as though he had seen it a hundred times, as though it meant nothing, as though the face in the photograph was not the first thing he had ever been forbidden to know about her.

"I can do that," he said.

She looked at him for a moment longer. Then she nodded, once, and returned to the kitchen.

He stayed in the doorway.

The photograph was still on the table.

He did not look at it.

---

Mr Stephen arrived at the hour he had named.

Not early. Not late. Exactly when he had said he would come, which was the kind of precision that could not be accused and could not be trusted. He stood at the gate with the same notebook, the same reasonable manner, the same umbrella that was not needed because the morning was dry.

The protagonist opened the gate.

"Good morning," Mr Stephen said.

"Good morning."

"I received your message."

The protagonist nodded. He had not sent a message. Obianuju had sent it — a boy from the lane, a folded note, no signature. The note had said only that there was something Mr Stephen should see before the next inspection. It had not said what.

Mr Stephen waited for an explanation. The protagonist did not give one, because he had been told not to explain the house to him.

"Inside," the protagonist said.

Mr Stephen followed him.

The front room was arranged the way Obianuju had arranged it — the chairs where they belonged, the mat shaken, the rent book on the lower shelf, the red thread on the table beside the photograph. The photograph was still face-down. The red thread lay beside it in a straight line, as if it had been placed there to measure something.

Obianuju was standing by the window. She did not sit. She did not offer tea. She stood the way she stood during the hour — present in the body, absent somewhere else — but the hour was hours away, and she was not absent. She was waiting.

Mr Stephen saw the photograph.

He did not reach for it. He looked at it the way he looked at everything — without appearing to look, without missing anything.

"You have something to show me," he said.

"Yes."

She did not pick it up. She left it on the table, face-down, and she let him see that she was leaving it there. That was the first move. Not the photograph. The refusal to hand it to him.

"It is a picture," she said. "Of the person who lived in this house before me."

Mr Stephen's face did not change. But something behind his eyes shifted — not surprise, not recognition, but a kind of confirmation, as though he had been told a thing he had already suspected.

"The person who lived here before you," he said.

"Yes."

"The one who left the cold."

She did not answer. She did not need to. The silence was the answer.

Mr Stephen looked at the photograph again. He did not touch it.

"May I?" he asked.

She nodded.

He picked it up.

He turned it over.

The protagonist saw Mr Stephen's face change. Not much. A man like Mr Stephen did not give his face away cheaply. But something in the set of his jaw shifted, and his thumb moved across the edge of the photograph once, slowly, as though he were reading something the paper had not printed.

He looked at it for a long time.

Longer than courtesy required.

Longer than a man looks at a photograph he does not recognise.

The protagonist stood at the door, as he had been told. He did not look at the photograph. He looked at Mr Stephen's hands — the way they held the frame, the way the thumb moved across the edge, the way the fingers adjusted the angle to catch the light. He read the photograph through the hands that held it. Mr Stephen had seen something he recognised.

"Who is she?" Mr Stephen asked.

Obianuju did not answer at once. She walked to the table. She stood beside the photograph, not touching it, not looking at it. She looked at Mr Stephen.

"Her name is not on the photograph."

"No," Mr Stephen said. "But you know it."

"Yes."

"Will you tell me?"

"No."

Mr Stephen considered this. He looked at the photograph again. His thumb moved across the edge once more.

"She is the reason the house is the way it is," he said.

It was not a question.

Obianuju did not answer.

Mr Stephen set the photograph down carefully, the way a man sets down a thing he has decided to believe. He did not put it back face-down. He left it face-up, the woman looking at the ceiling, the house behind her visible to anyone who cared to look.

"Thank you for showing me," he said.

He did not put the photograph down immediately. He held it a moment longer, his thumb still on the edge, and the protagonist understood that Mr Stephen was deciding how much to ask for next.

"May I ask where she went?"

Obianuju did not answer at once. "She did not say."

"And you did not look for her?"

"It was not my place to look."

Mr Stephen nodded. He set the photograph down carefully, the way a man sets down a thing he has decided to believe. He did not put it back face-down. He left it face-up, the woman looking at the ceiling, the house behind her visible to anyone who cared to look.

"I will not need to inspect the room today," he said.

The sentence was quiet. It was precise. It was a gift, and like all gifts from Mr Stephen, it had a string attached that would only become visible when pulled.

Obianuju did not thank him. She waited.

"But I will need to return after the next rain. The brace will not hold forever. And when I return, I will need to see the full extent of the damp."

"The full extent."

"Yes."

He did not say "the room." He said "the full extent of the damp." That was how he kept his hands clean while pressure continued.

Obianuju looked at him. Her face was unreadable.

"You will see what needs to be seen."

"That is all I am asking."

It was not all he was asking. They both knew it. But the photograph had bought them time, and time was the only currency that mattered now.

He stood up. He closed his notebook. He walked toward the door.

The protagonist stood aside to let him pass.

At the threshold, Mr Stephen stopped.

"The roof," he said.

"Yes."

"The brace. The board. The damp. Those are still there."

"Yes."

"They will need to be addressed."

"Yes."

He looked at the protagonist. His face was unreadable.

"But I understand now why you have been careful."

He did not say what he understood. He did not need to. The photograph had given him a story, and a story, even a partial one, was easier to carry than a mystery.

He walked away.

The protagonist stood at the gate and watched him go. The morning was ordinary. The market was waking. The sea was doing what the sea always did. And a man had just been given a piece of Obianuju's history and had walked away satisfied, not because he believed it, but because it was useful.

---

He went inside.

Obianuju was still standing at the table. The photograph was face-up. She was looking at it the way she had looked at it the night before — not with fear, not with anger, but with the attention of a woman who had learned that small things could carry more weight than large ones.

"He took it," the protagonist said.

"He took the story."

"Will it be enough?"

She looked at him. Her back had settled into the shape it took before she told him something she would rather have kept. The photograph lay before her like something already given.

"It will be enough until it is not."

"When will that be?"

"When he shows the photograph to someone who recognises the woman in it."

He looked at the photograph. The woman was still there, unsmiling, standing in front of a house that was not this house. She looked familiar in the way that faces from old photographs always look familiar — they belong to a time you did not live through.

"Who is she?" he asked.

Obianuju was quiet for a long moment. Then she said, "She was the woman who lived here before the woman who sold the house to Mr Stephen."

"The woman with the blue door said the cold had been there before her."

"Yes."

"So this woman — "

"She is the one the cold was waiting for."

The sentence found the seam in him and opened it wide.

"The cold was waiting for her," he said.

"Yes."

"Not for you."

"No."

"Then why — "

She looked at him. Her face was not unkind. Her shoulders carried the shape of someone who had held onto the question longer than she had held onto anything else.

"Because when she left, the cold did not leave with her. It stayed. And it was still waiting when I arrived. It thought I was her."

He stood very still.

"Does it still think that?"

She looked at the photograph. Her hand moved toward it, then stopped. She did not touch it.

"I do not know," she said. "That is what I have been trying to find out."

She was quiet for a moment. Then she said, "I found the photograph in the back of the shelf, behind the cloth pouch, the first week I moved in. It was wrapped in a piece of paper with no name on it. Just the photograph. Folded once. Hidden as though someone had wanted it to stay hidden and had not had time to take it with them."

He looked at her.

"You have had it since the first week."

"Yes."

"And you did not tell me."

"No."

"Why?"

She looked at him. In her silence was the weight of something she had been carrying since before he arrived — something that sat in her shoulders and her hands and the way her eyes did not flinch from his.

"Because I did not know whether the woman in the photograph was the one the cold was waiting for, or the one who had escaped it. I did not know whether keeping the photograph would protect me or make me visible. I did not know whether the cold knew I had it. And I was afraid that if I told you about it, you would want to use it the way you wanted to use everything — as a tool, as a key, as something to open the door with."

He said nothing.

"Tonight," she said, "I am still not sure. But I know that keeping it hidden was no longer protecting anything. And giving it to Mr Stephen was the only way to keep him from looking for something else."

She picked up the photograph. She looked at it one last time. Then she placed it back in the cloth pouch and closed the drawstring.

"He will come back," she said. "After the next rain. He will ask to see the full extent of the damp. And when he does, the photograph will not be enough to stop him."

"Then what will be?"

She looked at him. Her face was unreadable.

"I do not know yet. But I have until the next rain to find out."

---

Kene arrived in the late afternoon, after the photograph had been returned to the cloth pouch, after the red thread had been coiled and put away, after the table had been wiped clean of everything except the ordinary objects of the day.

He came with bread and a question he did not ask until he had eaten half of it.

"He took it," Kene said.

"Yes."

"He took the story."

"Yes."

Kene tore a piece of bread and chewed it slowly.

"That is good. It is also terrible. Because now he has something to verify."

"He does not know who the woman is."

"He does not need to know who she is. He only needs to know that she existed. And that you have a photograph of her. And that you were willing to show it to him."

The protagonist looked at him.

"What does that give him?"

Kene finished the bread. He wiped his hands on his trousers.

"It gives him proof that you are afraid of what he might find. Not of the inspection. Of something older. He does not know what it is. But he knows you are willing to give him a piece of your history to keep him from finding it. And that knowledge is worth more than any photograph."

The protagonist said nothing.

"Did you see it?" Kene asked.

"Yes."

"What did you see?"

"A woman. In front of a house. Not this house."

"And?"

"And that is all."

Kene looked at him. "That is all you saw, or that is all you are willing to say?"

The protagonist hesitated. The question was fair, and because it was fair he disliked it.

"That is all I saw. She did not show it to me. She left it face-down. I saw it when Mr Stephen turned it over."

"And you looked."

"Yes."

"For how long?"

"A few seconds."

Kene nodded slowly. "A few seconds. That is how long you had to see the face of the woman the cold was waiting for. And in those few seconds, you saw a house that was not this house, and a woman who was not smiling, and you did not ask to see it again."

"I asked who she was. She told me."

"She told you what she wanted you to know."

The protagonist looked at him. "You think she is hiding something."

"I think she is protecting something. That is different. And I think you are trying to turn her protection into intimacy because it is easier than sitting with the fact that she still does not trust you with the full story."

The protagonist said nothing. Kene was right, and the rightness of it sat between them like a thing that could not be taken back.

Kene stood up. He walked to the shelf where the cloth pouch hung. He did not touch it. He looked at it the way a man looks at a thing he has decided not to open.

"She gave him a face," Kene said. "That is what she did. She gave him a face to attach to the cold. Now he has a story he can carry. A story he can repeat. A story he can use."

"Is that bad?"

Kene turned to face him.

"It depends on what he does with the face."

---

That night, after Obianuju had gone to bed, he sat in the front room with the lamp burning low. The cloth pouch was on the shelf. The photograph was inside it. The red thread was coiled beside it. The cold was at the edges of the room, waiting, patient, unhurried.

He thought of the woman in the photograph. He did not know her name. He did not know whether she was alive or dead. He did not know whether she had left the house because she was afraid, or because she was brave, or because she had understood something about the cold that Obianuju was still trying to learn.

He thought of what Obianuju had said — that the cold had been waiting for that woman, and that when she left, the cold had stayed, and that it had thought Obianuju was her.

He thought of what it meant to be mistaken for someone else by something that did not know the difference.

He thought of the photograph, now in Mr Stephen's mind, now a story he could carry, now a face he could use.

He stood up.

He walked to the no-colour door and put his hand on the handle. The handle was cold. Not the cold of metal left in a draught. The cold of something that had been touched by a room it did not belong to.

He did not open the door.

He stood there with his hand on the cold handle. The door was not the door he needed to open. The one he needed to open was the one that would let him stop waiting for Mr Stephen to use the photograph and start preparing for what would happen when he did.

He took his hand off the handle.

He went back to the front room.

He sat down.

The cloth pouch was on the shelf.

The cold was at the edges.

And in the morning, the house would begin to feel the weight of what had been given away — not the photograph, not the story, but the knowledge that Obianuju had been willing to give them. That was what Mr Stephen had gained. Not a face. Not a name. The knowledge that she was afraid enough to surrender a piece of her history.

And that knowledge, unlike the photograph, could not be returned to the pouch.

MOVEMENT II DRAFT. Ch.25: The Story He Could Use. Function: the photograph story has left the table and entered the world. Mr Stephen uses it reasonably. Obianuju chooses under worsened conditions. The protagonist acts without owning. The room remains closed but the cost moves elsewhere. No new Trance. No new Voice line. Motifs: story/face/table/hands/threshold/rain. Target 3,800-4,800.
